# additional unfinished discussed domains

> Compiled canonical and source material for this pillar. Review and edit for tone and finalization.

## Intent and scope

This pillar aggregates rules with scope codes: AGR,SYS. Content below includes canonical rules, linked policy items, explicit record links, and all matching source excerpts from the main and brainstorm logs.

## System rules compliance

All entries must be validated against system_rules.md: human oversight for high-risk decisions, non-fragmentation of rights, provenance, and layered oversight for centralized risks.

> 627:Perfect. This is where tax policy stops feeling like a punishment maze for ordinary people and starts becoming a real public system.
> 646: IRS modernization + public filing systems
> 651:TAX-SMP-004 | Tax systems for ordinary individuals must be simple, low-burden, understandable, and designed so compliance does not require professional assistance in routine cases.
> 661:TAX-SMP-008 | Individuals with straightforward income, withholding, and deduction profiles should not be required to navigate complex filing systems or purchase commercial tax-preparation products.
> 663:TAX-SMP-009 | The government must provide free public filing systems that are easy to use, transparent, and available to all taxpayers.
> 665:TAX-SMP-010 | Public tax-filing systems must be designed to minimize error, maximize transparency, and clearly show how tax obligations are calculated.
> 675:TAX-PER-001 | Personal income tax systems must be progressive overall and structured so those with greater ability to pay contribute more.
> 679:TAX-PER-003 | Personal tax systems must protect low-income households from tax burdens that undermine basic living standards.
> 683:TAX-PAY-001 | Payroll and labor-related tax systems must be structured so they do not disproportionately burden workers relative to owners of concentrated wealth and passive capital.
> 685:TAX-PAY-002 | Payroll tax systems should be reviewed and rebalanced where necessary to reduce undue burden on wage earners and smaller employers.
> 687:TAX-PAY-003 | Funding for social insurance systems must remain stable and durable while ensuring the contribution structure is fair across labor and capital income.
> 693:TAX-PER-005 | Common deductions and credits with broad public purpose should be incorporated into simple filing systems automatically wherever feasible.
> 699:This is where the system gets sharper.
> 703:TAX-WTH-008 | Tax systems must apply heightened reporting, audit, and anti-avoidance scrutiny to high-net-worth individuals and complex personal wealth structures.
> 723:TAX-SMP-013 | Public tax systems must be accessible to people with disabilities, limited English proficiency, low digital literacy, and limited financial sophistication.
> 725:TAX-SMP-014 | Tax notices, filings, and payment systems must be written in clear language and supported by accessible public assistance.
> 739:TAX-ADM-001 | Tax administration systems should use automation, data matching, and modern technology to simplify filing for ordinary taxpayers and improve enforcement against complex evasion.
> 741:TAX-ADM-002 | Automated tax-administration systems must be transparent, reviewable, and subject to human appeal where they materially affect taxpayer obligations or enforcement outcomes.
> 753:TAX-DMJ-003 | The tax system must be visibly fair in practice so that ordinary taxpayers are not asked to comply with burdens from which wealthy actors routinely escape.
> 755:TAX-DMJ-004 | Simplicity, fairness, and enforceability are co-equal design goals of the tax system and may not be traded away in favor of complexity that benefits powerful interests.
> 764:wealthy people game the system through complexity
> 828:`ADM-SYS-001 | Administrative agencies are legitimate constitutional instruments of democratic governance and must exist to implement, enforce, and adapt public-interest law in areas requiring expertise, continuity, and active oversight.`
> 830:`ADM-SYS-002 | Agencies must be strong enough to regulate complex modern systems effectively, but constrained by transparency, accountability, review, and anti-capture protections.`
> 832:`ADM-SYS-003 | Agency design must prevent both political sabotage and unaccountable bureaucratic abuse.`
> 874:`ADM-FND-001 | Agencies charged with protecting rights, public safety, markets, health, labor, environment, or democratic systems must receive stable and adequate funding sufficient to carry out their lawful mission.`
> 900:`ADM-ENF-003 | Repeated or systemic violations must trigger escalated review and stronger remedies rather than routine settlement or symbolic penalties alone.`
> 902:`ADM-ENF-004 | Enforcement systems may not rely solely on fines where ongoing behavior requires operational, structural, or leadership remedies.`
> 906:`ADM-ADJ-001 | Agency adjudication systems must provide clear notice, records access, explanation of decisions, and meaningful appeal rights.`
> 950:`ADM-OVR-003 | Findings of systemic agency failure, capture, abuse, or chronic non-enforcement must trigger mandatory corrective review.`
> 970:`TAX-IRS-002 | IRS modernization must prioritize easy filing for ordinary taxpayers, high-end enforcement, anti-fraud capacity, and modern data systems.`
> 972:## Filing and public systems
> 974:`TAX-IRS-003 | The IRS must provide free, direct, public tax filing systems for individuals and small businesses.`
> 978:`TAX-IRS-005 | Taxpayer-facing systems must be accessible, multilingual, disability-compatible, and usable without specialized tax software.`
> 982:`TAX-IRS-006 | IRS enforcement systems must prioritize sophisticated high-revenue avoidance and evasion rather than focusing primarily on low-income, low-complexity cases.`
> 984:`TAX-IRS-007 | IRS data systems should support detection of shell structures, beneficial ownership concealment, high-risk transfer patterns, and abusive complexity.`
> 994:`TAX-IRS-010 | Tax administration must visibly demonstrate that high-wealth and high-complexity cases are enforced seriously in order to preserve legitimacy of the tax system.`
> 1002:`FIN-SYS-001 | Public finance must prioritize durable funding of essential public goods, long-term resilience, and reduction of systemic risk.`
> 1004:`FIN-SYS-002 | Budget policy may not rely on chronic underinvestment in essential systems while preserving tax advantages or subsidies for concentrated private gain.`
> 1008:`FIN-PRI-001 | Public budgets must prioritize healthcare, education, housing, labor enforcement, environmental protection, infrastructure, disability access, democratic systems, and public safety rooted in rights and accountability.`
> 1014:`FIN-PRI-003 | Essential rights-protecting and public-interest systems may not be systematically weakened through austerity where doing so predictably creates larger long-term harm.`

> 1085:TAX-ENV-001 | Environmental tax policy must internalize environmental harms that are currently externalized onto the public, future generations, and ecosystems.
> 1086:TAX-ENV-002 | Environmental taxation must be structured to reduce pollution, waste, emissions, ecosystem damage, and resource depletion while supporting a just transition.
> 1090:TAX-ENV-005 | Environmental tax systems must not rely on unverifiable offsets, accounting games, or market abstractions that allow polluters to avoid real reductions.
> 1092:TAX-ENV-007 | Environmental tax systems must distinguish between productive necessity and avoidable pollution while preserving a clear path toward decarbonization.
> 1094:TAX-ENV-008 | Water extraction and intensive water use may be subject to taxation, fees, or escalating charges where use patterns threaten sustainability, public access, or ecosystem health.
> 1097:TAX-ENV-011 | Water taxation and fee systems must not undermine basic household access to safe water or create hardship for essential personal use.
> 1100:TAX-ENV-013 | Tax and fee systems may be used to discourage plastics, toxic byproducts, non-recyclable packaging, persistent waste, and environmentally destructive production methods.
> 1101:TAX-ENV-014 | Environmental taxes must account for lifecycle harm, including extraction, production, use, disposal, and leakage into ecosystems.
> 1104:TAX-ENV-016 | Tax advantages may be used to support repair, remanufacturing, refurbishment, reuse systems, and circular-economy infrastructure.
> 1107:TAX-ENV-018 | Environmental tax systems must include strong anti-evasion rules so firms may not avoid liability through offshoring, shell structures, accounting manipulation, or supply-chain opacity.
> 1111:TAX-ENV-021 | Revenue from environmental taxation should support environmental cleanup, climate resilience, public health protection, ecosystem restoration, and just-transition programs.
> 1113:TAX-ENV-023 | Environmental tax systems must be designed so that working households and low-income communities are not unfairly burdened relative to major industrial polluters and concentrated private gain.
> 1115:TAX-ENV-024 | Environmental tax systems must be transparent, publicly understandable, and based on standardized reporting, measurable criteria, and auditable data.
> 1121:TAX-ENV-029 | Repeated or systemic environmental tax violations must trigger escalating penalties, corrective orders, audit expansion, and structural remedies where necessary.

### Excerpt 1191

```
Good place to start. Across industry, standards bodies, civil-society groups, and international organizations, there is a pretty strong overlapping consensus on a few AI basics: high-risk systems need governance before deployment; decisions affecting rights or access need transparency, auditability, and human accountability; surveillance and military uses deserve stricter rules than ordinary commercial uses; and AI’s environmental footprint is now large enough to treat as a policy issue, not an externality. NIST’s AI RMF and Generative AI Profile emphasize governance, testing, documentation, monitoring, and misuse controls; UNESCO and the OECD frame AI around human rights, transparency, accountability, and democratic values; and WHO explicitly treats AI in health as a safety, equity, and governance problem rather than just a product problem.
```

### Excerpt 1192

```
On military AI, the strongest outside signal is that fully or highly autonomous force decisions are widely treated as a special category. The ICRC argues autonomous weapons raise immediate humanitarian concerns and calls for prohibitions and tight regulation, especially where systems cannot be predictably constrained under international humanitarian law. Even some industry-facing safety frameworks now talk in terms of capability thresholds, staged deployment, and restricted release when risk becomes too high.
```

### Excerpt 1193

```
For industry insiders, the notable pattern is not that they all agree politically, but that many of them are now talking in governance language you can use: restricted deployment for frontier capabilities, safety thresholds, auditing, democratic accountability, and coordination with public institutions. Anthropic’s Responsible Scaling Policy is an example of a company saying some capabilities require escalating safeguards; OpenAI’s recent industrial-policy paper also talks in terms of controllability, transparency, trust, and democratic alignment; DeepMind’s public materials emphasize risk assessment, privacy, misuse mitigation, and frontier safety. You do not need to trust any one company to notice that even insiders now describe advanced AI as requiring institutional guardrails rather than pure market freedom.
```

### Excerpt 1194

```
1. Human rights and civil liberties come first.
```

### Excerpt 1195

```
AI systems may not override due process, privacy, equal protection, bodily autonomy, or access to rights-bearing public systems. This is consistent with UNESCO, OECD, and NIST-style risk governance.
```

### Excerpt 1196

```
4. AI systems must be auditable, contestable, and attributable.
```

### Excerpt 1197

```
If AI materially affects someone’s rights, safety, or opportunities, there must be traceability, documentation, human accountability, and a way to challenge outcomes. That is straight down the middle of the major standards conversation.
```

### Excerpt 1198

```
TEC-AI-001 AI systems that materially affect rights, liberty, healthcare, education, employment, benefits, or policing must be subject to heightened governance and audit requirements.
```

### Excerpt 1199

```
TEC-AI-002 No fully automated decisions in critical domains without meaningful human review, clear accountability, and a right to contest outcomes.
```

### Excerpt 1200

```
TEC-AI-003 Military AI must not be permitted to make uncontrolled lethal targeting or force decisions without strict human accountability and legal constraints.
```

### Excerpt 1201

```
TEC-SUR-001 Ban or sharply restrict AI-powered mass surveillance, including persistent public-space tracking, predictive policing, and suspicion scoring.
```

### Excerpt 1202

```
TEC-MHC-001 AI mental-health tools may assist but must not replace qualified clinical judgment in diagnosis, crisis response, or high-risk treatment decisions.
```

### Excerpt 1203

```
TEC-ENV-001 Large AI models and data centers must meet strict transparency standards for energy, water, emissions, and e-waste impacts.
```

### Excerpt 1204

```
TEC-ALG-001 People must have the right to know when AI materially influences ranking, eligibility, moderation, triage, or access decisions.
```

### Excerpt 1205

```
TEC-ALG-002 Covered AI systems must be independently auditable for bias, safety, manipulation, and reliability.
```

### Excerpt 1206

```
Your UK/NHS/Cass concern can also be captured cleanly without turning the checkpoint into a fight over one report: keep it as a system-failure rule that healthcare or AI-health policy must not permit selective withholding of relevant evidence, unsupported conclusions, or politically distorted scientific interpretation. That principle is already well aligned with the healthcare scientific-integrity items we captured earlier.
```

```

### Excerpt 1664

```

### Excerpt 1666

```
HLT-AI-007C|HLT|AI|Human review must occur prior to any denial decision and may not be deferred to post-decision appeal processes|MISSING

```
B. Human review before harm
```

### Excerpt 2005

```
TEC-FIN-003|TEC|FIN|Any decision that would deny restrict or materially worsen access to credit or lending must be made directly and independently by a qualified human reviewer before harm occurs|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2006

```
C. No opaque underwriting
```

### Excerpt 2007

```
TEC-FIN-004|TEC|FIN|AI systems used in underwriting or creditworthiness assessments may not rely on opaque or unexplainable criteria that materially affect outcomes|MISSING
```

```

### Excerpt 2010

```

### Excerpt 2012

TEC-FIN-006|TEC|FIN|AI systems used in lending underwriting pricing or claims decisions must be regularly audited for disparate impact and discriminatory outcomes|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2013

```
4. Insurance Decisions
```

### Excerpt 2014

```
A. No AI-only denials of claims or coverage
```

### Excerpt 2015

```
TEC-FIN-007|TEC|FIN|AI systems may not independently deny restrict reduce or terminate insurance coverage or claims without direct and independent human review|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2016

```
B. No rubber-stamping
```

### Excerpt 2017

```
TEC-FIN-008|TEC|FIN|Human reviewers in insurance decisions may not rely solely on AI-generated recommendations and must exercise independent judgment|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2018

```
C. AI may assist approvals, not denials
```

### Excerpt 2019

```
TEC-FIN-009|TEC|FIN|AI systems may be used to assist or expedite approval of insurance claims or coverage but may not be used as the primary basis for denial restriction or reduction|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2020

```
D. No negative inference
```

### Excerpt 2021

```
TEC-FIN-010|TEC|FIN|Absence of AI approval or recommendation may not be used as evidence or implicit justification for denial restriction or delay of insurance claims or coverage|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2079

```
🧠 1. SYSTEM-LEVEL RULES (CROSS-DOMAIN)
```

### Excerpt 2080

```
A. No negative inference / denial bias (globalized)
```

### Excerpt 2081

```
SYS-AI-001|SYS|AI|Absence of AI approval recommendation or prioritization may not be used as evidence or implicit justification for denial restriction or adverse decisions in any domain|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2082

```
B. Independent human decision before harm
```

### Excerpt 2083

```
SYS-AI-002|SYS|AI|Any decision that materially harms access to rights opportunities services or benefits must be made directly and independently by a qualified human decision-maker prior to harm|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2084

```
C. No “fast lane / slow lane” bias
```

### Excerpt 2085

```
SYS-AI-003|SYS|AI|AI systems may not create or reinforce structural bias where approvals are accelerated while non-prioritized cases are delayed deprioritized or more likely to be denied|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2086

```
D. Symmetry & fairness requirement
```

### Excerpt 2087

```
SYS-AI-004|SYS|AI|AI-assisted decision systems must be designed to maintain fairness symmetry and equal consideration across all cases regardless of AI prioritization or recommendation|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2088

```
E. Mandatory bias detection & mitigation
```

### Excerpt 2089

```
SYS-AI-005|SYS|AI|AI systems used in decision-making must include explicit mechanisms to detect measure and mitigate bias and must be used to reduce rather than reinforce existing inequities|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2090

```
F. Human accountability cannot be displaced
```

### Excerpt 2091

```
SYS-AI-006|SYS|AI|Use of AI systems does not reduce or transfer legal ethical or professional accountability from human decision-makers|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2092

```
G. Domain-level system rules requirement
```

### Excerpt 2093

```
SYS-AI-007|SYS|AI|Each policy domain or pillar must define its own AI system rules consistent with these overarching principles and adapted to domain-specific risks|MISSING
```

```

### Excerpt 2110

```

### Excerpt 2125

```
SYS-AI-002|SYS|AI|Any decision that materially harms restricts or denies rights opportunities or access must be made through independent human judgment and may not rely on absence of AI support|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2126

🔧 Add System Rule: No Negative Inference
```

### Excerpt 2127

```

```

### Excerpt 2128

🔧 Add System Rule: Queue Fairness
```

### Excerpt 2129

```

```

### Excerpt 2132

```

🔧 Add System Rule: Continuous Audit
```

### Excerpt 2134

```
SYS-AI-006|SYS|AI|Decision-making systems using AI must be continuously audited for bias disparate impact and unfair outcomes with corrective action required|MISSING

### Excerpt 2322

```
What system rules are, and why they matter
```

### Excerpt 2323

```
A system rule is a cross-domain rule that applies to multiple pillars and prevents inconsistency, loopholes, or structural drift.
```

### Excerpt 2324

```
Instead of only writing isolated policies inside each domain, system rules create shared constraints and design principles that all domains must obey. They serve as:
```

### Excerpt 2325

```
For example, if one domain says AI may help with approvals, but another domain accidentally allows that to create a denial bias, a system rule can override both and say:
```

### Excerpt 2326

```
AI may not create asymmetrical systems that accelerate approvals while making non-prioritized cases slower, more scrutinized, or more likely to be denied.
```

### Excerpt 2327

```
That kind of rule should not live only inside healthcare or insurance. It should live at the system level and then be applied within every relevant domain.
```

### Excerpt 2328

```
System rules are especially important in this project because the platform is trying to solve:
```

### Excerpt 2329

```
system rules: universal cross-domain rules
```

### Excerpt 2330

```
domain rules: rules tailored to the specific risks of each pillar or section
```

### Excerpt 2331

```
Current system rules
```

### Excerpt 2332

```
Foundational system rules
```

```
Platform is both a policy platform and a long-term strategy
```

```

```

```

```

```

### Excerpt 2344

Geographic equality / anti-fragmentation rules
```

### Excerpt 2345

```
Concept: geography must not determine rights, care, or access to core systems
```

### Excerpt 2346

```
Concept: systems must be structured according to their primary failure mode: fragmentation or capture
```

### Excerpt 2347

```
High-risk systems must not be fully centralized
```

### Excerpt 2348

```
Elections remain primarily state and local administered
```

### Excerpt 2349

```
Federal government may set standards but should not directly control election administration
```

Multiple independent oversight layers are required
```

Safeguards are needed against both state suppression and federal manipulation
```

### Excerpt 2352

```
AI system rules
```

### Excerpt 2353

```
AI-assisted systems must not create asymmetrical processes that speed approvals while making everything else slower or more denial-prone
```

### Excerpt 2354

```
Harmful decisions must be made through independent human judgment before harm occurs
```

### Excerpt 2355

```
Absence of AI recommendation cannot count against a person
```

### Excerpt 2356

```
Queue fairness must be preserved even when AI prioritizes some cases
```

### Excerpt 2357

AI systems must include bias detection and bias-reduction mechanisms
```

### Excerpt 2358

```
Human accountability cannot be displaced by AI
```

```
Every domain should define its own domain-specific AI system rules

### Excerpt 2527

```

### Excerpt 2528

```

```

### Excerpt 2529

```

### Excerpt 2530

```
Decentralization of power
```

### Excerpt 2531

```

### Excerpt 2532

```

### Excerpt 2533

```
Structural fairness and anti-rigging
```

### Excerpt 2534

```

```

### Excerpt 2535

```

```

### Excerpt 2536

```
Systems must function under stress, resist exploitation, and remain stable over time.
```

### Excerpt 2537

```
Drives: institutional design, fail-safes, long-term policy planning.
```

### Excerpt 2538

```
Outdated laws, structures, and language must be updated to reflect current realities and eliminate ambiguity or exploitation.
```

### Excerpt 2539

```
Drives: constitutional modernization, statutory reform, simplification and clarification of legal language.
```

### Excerpt 2540

```
Future-proofing and adaptive governance
```

### Excerpt 2541

```
Systems must be designed to evolve alongside technological, social, and economic change.
```

### Excerpt 2542

```
Drives: flexible regulatory frameworks, update mechanisms, long-term policy adaptability.
```

### Excerpt 2543

```
Proactive governance and risk prevention
```

### Excerpt 2544

```
Governance should anticipate risks and address them before harm occurs, rather than reacting after failure.
```

### Excerpt 2545

```
Drives: early intervention systems, risk modeling, preventative policy design.
```

### Excerpt 2546

```
Public-interest–aligned governance
```

### Excerpt 2547

```
Systems must prioritize outcomes that benefit the public, not private interests.
```

### Excerpt 2548

```
Drives: regulatory reform, public infrastructure, anti-capture measures.
```

### Excerpt 2549

```
Systems must be actively tested against misuse, failure, and bad actors.
```

### Excerpt 2550

```
Drives: policy review frameworks, red-teaming, oversight audits.
```

```
→ prevents the system from becoming outdated again

### Excerpt 2557

Absolutely. Here’s a cleaner, collaborator-friendly version that reflects your framing, uses “I” instead of “we,” reduces AI recency bias, shortens the system-rules section, and folds the summary, counts, coverage estimates, and checklist into one readable status document.

### Excerpt 2558

```
concentration and abuse of political, economic, and technological power
```

### Excerpt 2559

```
corruption, opacity, and lack of accountability
```

### Excerpt 2560

```
erosion, ambiguity, or rollback of rights and protections
```

### Excerpt 2561

```
unequal treatment under law and unequal access to core systems
```

### Excerpt 2562

```
outdated constitutional and legal language that is too vague, too easy to exploit, or too open to bad-faith interpretation
```

### Excerpt 2563

```
patchwork systems that create different classes of rights and access depending on geography
```

### Excerpt 2564

```
systemic unfairness, rigging, and capture
```

### Excerpt 2565

```
economic instability and inability for ordinary people to build durable lives
```

### Excerpt 2566

```
failure of laws and institutions to keep pace with technology and modern infrastructure
```

### Excerpt 2567

```
lack of proactive governance and overreliance on reactive, after-the-fact fixes
```

### Excerpt 2568

```
failure to define rights and protections positively, clearly, and durably
```

### Excerpt 2569

```
failure to modernize institutions for current and future technological realities
```

### Excerpt 2589

```
What system rules are
```

### Excerpt 2590

```
A system rule is a cross-domain rule that applies across multiple pillars and prevents inconsistency, loopholes, structural drift, or domain-by-domain contradiction.
```

### Excerpt 2591

```
System rules exist so that:
```

### Excerpt 2592

```
a rule in one domain does not accidentally undermine another
```

### Excerpt 2593

```
implementation details do not quietly violate core principles
```

### Excerpt 2594

```
policy remains consistent across healthcare, justice, elections, technology, education, labor, and other domains
```

### Excerpt 2595

```
recurring failure modes are addressed once at the architecture level instead of repeatedly patching them in isolated sections
```

### Excerpt 2596

```
Current system-rule concepts in use
```

### Excerpt 2597

```
At a high level, the current system rules and system-rule families include:
```

### Excerpt 2598

```
foundational mission / architecture rules
```

### Excerpt 2599

```
geographic equality / anti-fragmentation rules
```

### Excerpt 2600

```
federalism-balance rules
```

### Excerpt 2601

```
decision-integrity / anti-denial-bias rules
```

### Excerpt 2602

```
bias-detection and fairness rules
```

### Excerpt 2603

```
accountability and human-review rules
```

### Excerpt 2604

```
domain-specific system rules requirement
```

### Excerpt 2605

```
Current high-level system-rule ideals
```

### Excerpt 2606

```
The current system-rule structure is built around ideas like:
```

### Excerpt 2607

```
geography should not determine access to rights or core services
```

### Excerpt 2608

```
systems should be structured based on whether the main risk is fragmentation or capture
```

### Excerpt 2609

```
AI must not create denial bias, asymmetry, or hidden harm
```

### Excerpt 2610

```
harmful decisions must involve accountable human judgment
```

### Excerpt 2611

```
absence of algorithmic approval must not count against a person
```

### Excerpt 2612

```
fairness and queue integrity must be preserved
```

### Excerpt 2613

```
AI should be used to detect and reduce bias, not reinforce it
```

### Excerpt 2614

```
every major domain should define its own domain-level system rules consistent with the overarching ones
```

If genuinely sentient AI systems ever exist, I am open to and supportive of:

### Excerpt 2717

```
Judicial workflow
```

### Excerpt 2718

```
TEC-JUD-008|TEC|JUD|Judges and court staff may not rely on AI systems to make final determinations of fact law credibility or case outcome|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2719

```
TEC-JUD-009|TEC|JUD|AI may be used to assist with clerical summarization research and document organization only under clear disclosure and human verification requirements|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2720

```
TEC-JUD-010|TEC|JUD|Courts must disclose material use of AI in opinion drafting legal analysis record summarization or case management where it could affect litigants’ rights or case outcomes|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2721

```
TEC-JUD-011|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to prioritize dockets motions or hearings in ways that create unfairness bias or unequal access to timely justice|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2722

```
TEC-JUD-012|TEC|JUD|Any AI-assisted judicial workflow must preserve a human-authored accountable and reviewable chain of reasoning for consequential rulings|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2723

```
Evidentiary admissibility
```

### Excerpt 2724

```
TEC-JUD-013|TEC|JUD|AI-generated or AI-enhanced evidence must be authenticated with documented provenance methodology and chain of custody before admissibility|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2725

```
TEC-JUD-014|TEC|JUD|Parties must receive sufficient technical disclosure to challenge the reliability validity and limitations of AI-generated or AI-analyzed evidence|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2726

```
TEC-JUD-015|TEC|JUD|Courts may not admit AI outputs as expert-like evidence unless their methods are scientifically valid independently testable and subject to adversarial challenge|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2727

```
TEC-JUD-016|TEC|JUD|Synthetic media evidence must be presumptively treated as high-risk and require enhanced authentication standards before admission|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2728

```
TEC-JUD-017|TEC|JUD|If AI is used to analyze evidence all material logs model assumptions and transformation steps relevant to the output must be preservable and reviewable|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2729

```
Prosecutor / defender parity
```

### Excerpt 2730

```
TEC-JUD-018|TEC|JUD|If prosecutors or the state use AI systems in investigation charging discovery or litigation support defendants must have equal access to the relevant outputs disclosures and technical challenge mechanisms|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2731

```
TEC-JUD-019|TEC|JUD|Public defenders and defense counsel must be provided funding and access sufficient to avoid AI-driven asymmetry between prosecution and defense|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2732

```
TEC-JUD-020|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to pressure plea deals through opaque case scoring predicted conviction metrics or punishment leverage models|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2733

```
TEC-JUD-021|TEC|JUD|Courts must guard against prosecutorial advantage created by proprietary or undisclosed AI tools unavailable to defense|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2734

```
Jury-facing restrictions
```

### Excerpt 2735

```
TEC-JUD-022|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to profile rank influence or strategically shape juror selection based on behavioral psychological or demographic inference|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2736

```
TEC-JUD-023|TEC|JUD|AI-generated reconstructions simulations or visualizations shown to juries must be clearly disclosed as generated demonstrative material and subject to strict admissibility standards|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2737

```
TEC-JUD-024|TEC|JUD|Courts may not provide juries with AI-generated summaries interpretations or credibility assessments of testimony or evidence|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2738

```
TEC-JUD-025|TEC|JUD|Jury-facing use of AI must not distort the evidentiary record or create false impressions of certainty precision or neutrality|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2739

```
Dedupe / conflict check
```

### Excerpt 2740

```
⚖️ AI + JUSTICE / COURTS — TIGHTENED RULESET
```

### Excerpt 2741

```
1. Core Doctrine (New – anchors everything)
```

### Excerpt 2742

```
TEC-JUD-026|TEC|JUD|AI use in courts and legal proceedings must default to prohibition except where narrowly permitted for transparent auditable non-generative analytical purposes under strict human oversight|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2743

```
2. Ban on AI-Generated Evidence
```

### Excerpt 2744

```
A. Hard ban
```

### Excerpt 2745

```
TEC-JUD-027|TEC|JUD|AI-generated or AI-fabricated evidence including images video audio text or reconstructions is not admissible in legal proceedings|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2746

```
B. Ban on AI-enhanced evidence that alters meaning
```

### Excerpt 2747

```
TEC-JUD-028|TEC|JUD|AI-enhanced evidence that alters interpretation content or meaning of underlying evidence is not admissible|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2748

```
C. Narrow exception: analytical use only
```

### Excerpt 2749

```
TEC-JUD-029|TEC|JUD|AI may be used only for analytical purposes on existing evidence where it does not generate new content and does not alter underlying data|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2750

```
3. Strict Limits on AI Analysis of Evidence
```

### Excerpt 2751

```
A. No generative models
```

### Excerpt 2752

```
TEC-JUD-030|TEC|JUD|Generative AI systems including large language models may not be used for evidentiary analysis in legal proceedings|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2753

```
B. Preference for verifiable methods
```

### Excerpt 2754

```
TEC-JUD-031|TEC|JUD|Permitted analytical systems must use verifiable reproducible methods such as statistical or traditional machine learning models rather than generative systems|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2755

```
C. Full disclosure requirement
```

### Excerpt 2756

```
TEC-JUD-032|TEC|JUD|Any use of AI in evidence analysis must be explicitly disclosed including methods data inputs limitations and error rates|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2757

```
D. No black-box analysis
```

### Excerpt 2758

```
TEC-JUD-033|TEC|JUD|Black-box AI systems that cannot be meaningfully explained tested or challenged are not admissible in evidentiary contexts|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2759

```
4. No AI Experts
```

### Excerpt 2760

```
A. Ban on AI as expert witness
```

### Excerpt 2761

```
TEC-JUD-034|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be presented as expert witnesses or as authoritative sources in legal proceedings|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2762

```
B. Human experts only
```

### Excerpt 2763

```
TEC-JUD-035|TEC|JUD|All expert testimony must be provided by qualified human experts who are accountable for their conclusions|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2764

```
C. AI-assisted experts must disclose use
```

### Excerpt 2765

```
TEC-JUD-036|TEC|JUD|Human experts using AI-assisted analysis must disclose such use and remain fully responsible for all conclusions|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2766

```
5. Judicial Use Restrictions
```

### Excerpt 2767

```
A. Ban on generative AI for opinions
```

### Excerpt 2768

```
TEC-JUD-037|TEC|JUD|Judges and justices may not use generative AI systems to draft write or materially shape judicial opinions or rulings|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2769

```
B. No AI reasoning substitution
```

### Excerpt 2770

```
TEC-JUD-038|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not substitute for judicial reasoning legal analysis or interpretation of law|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2771

```
C. Limited clerical use only
```

### Excerpt 2772

```
TEC-JUD-039|TEC|JUD|AI may be used for limited clerical tasks such as document organization or formatting only when it does not affect legal reasoning or outcomes|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2773

```
6. Jury Protections (Tightened)
```

### Excerpt 2774

```
A. Ban on AI-generated courtroom materials
```

### Excerpt 2775

```
TEC-JUD-040|TEC|JUD|AI-generated reconstructions simulations or demonstrative materials may not be shown to juries|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2776

```
B. No AI summaries
```

### Excerpt 2777

```
TEC-JUD-041|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to summarize interpret or present evidence or testimony to juries|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2778

```
C. No AI influence on jurors
```

### Excerpt 2779

```
TEC-JUD-042|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to influence juror perception behavior or decision-making directly or indirectly|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2780

```
7. Confirmation Bias & Reliability Safeguards
```

### Excerpt 2781

```
A. Explicit bias risk recognition
```

### Excerpt 2782

```
TEC-JUD-043|TEC|JUD|Courts must recognize that AI systems can amplify confirmation bias hallucination and false certainty and must treat AI outputs as inherently high-risk|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2783

```
B. Elevated evidentiary standard
```

### Excerpt 2784

```
TEC-JUD-044|TEC|JUD|Any permitted AI-assisted analysis must meet elevated standards of reliability scrutiny and adversarial testing beyond traditional evidence|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2785

```
8. Adversarial Challenge Rights
```

### Excerpt 2786

```
A. Full right to challenge
```

### Excerpt 2787

```
TEC-JUD-045|TEC|JUD|All parties must have full rights to challenge any AI-assisted analysis including methodology assumptions and outputs|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2788

```
B. Reproducibility requirement
```

### Excerpt 2789

```
TEC-JUD-046|TEC|JUD|AI-assisted analysis must be reproducible by opposing parties using the same inputs and methods|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2790

```
9. Logging, Preservation, and Auditability
```

### Excerpt 2791

```
A. Full logging requirement
```

### Excerpt 2792

```
TEC-JUD-047|TEC|JUD|All AI-assisted evidentiary analysis must include preserved logs of inputs outputs and transformation steps|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2793

```
B. Audit access
```

### Excerpt 2794

```
TEC-JUD-048|TEC|JUD|Courts and opposing parties must have access to all materials necessary to audit AI-assisted analysis|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2802

```
⚠️ One design choice you just made (and it’s important)
```

### Excerpt 2803

```
Courts require a higher epistemic standard than most systems,
```

### Excerpt 2804

```
therefore AI must be more restricted here than elsewhere.
```

### Excerpt 2805

```
Absolutely. Let’s close the loop on AI + justice / courts by covering the places where abuse often hides in quieter, less glamorous systems:
```

### Excerpt 2806

```
family court
```

### Excerpt 2807

```
housing / eviction court
```

### Excerpt 2808

```
administrative tribunals
```

### Excerpt 2809

```
probation / parole
```

### Excerpt 2810

```
fines / fees / collections
```

### Excerpt 2811

```
legal aid access
```

### Excerpt 2812

```
court operations and record systems
```

### Excerpt 2813

```
TEC-JUD-049|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to determine custody visitation parental fitness or family placement outcomes without direct accountable human judicial decision-making|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2814

```
TEC-JUD-050|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not infer parental fitness abuse risk credibility or child welfare outcomes from behavioral proxies psychological profiling or opaque risk models|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2815

```
TEC-JUD-051|TEC|JUD|Family courts may not rely on AI-generated summaries recommendations or credibility assessments in matters involving custody abuse allegations or child welfare|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2816

```
TEC-JUD-052|TEC|JUD|Any AI-assisted tool used in family court administration must be fully disclosed and may not materially affect outcomes without adversarial challenge rights|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2817

```
2. Housing / Eviction Court
```

### Excerpt 2818

```
TEC-JUD-053|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to automate or materially drive eviction outcomes without direct human judicial review and full due process protections|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2819

```
TEC-JUD-054|TEC|JUD|Courts may not rely on AI-generated tenant risk scores rental-behavior predictions or opaque housing analytics in eviction or housing-access proceedings|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2820

```
TEC-JUD-055|TEC|JUD|Landlords and housing litigants using AI-assisted evidence or analytics in court must disclose such use and provide sufficient information for challenge and review|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2821

```
TEC-JUD-056|TEC|JUD|Housing courts must not use AI systems to accelerate case throughput in ways that reduce meaningful notice hearing access or tenant defense rights|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2822

```
3. Administrative Tribunals and Agency Adjudication
```

### Excerpt 2823

```
This matters because a lot of people experience “justice” through agencies, benefits systems, licensing boards, and hearings rather than traditional courts.
```

### Excerpt 2824

```
TEC-JUD-057|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not independently determine outcomes in administrative hearings involving benefits licensing immigration disability housing or employment rights|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2825

```
TEC-JUD-058|TEC|JUD|Administrative adjudication may not rely on opaque AI scoring or recommendation systems to determine credibility eligibility compliance or sanction outcomes|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2826

```
TEC-JUD-059|TEC|JUD|Parties in administrative proceedings have the right to know when AI materially influenced a recommendation record summary or proposed outcome|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2827

```
TEC-JUD-060|TEC|JUD|Administrative agencies must provide meaningful human review and appeal for any AI-influenced adverse decision|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2828

```
4. Probation / Parole / Community Supervision
```

### Excerpt 2829

```
This is a classic abuse zone for automated risk systems.
```

### Excerpt 2830

```
TEC-JUD-061|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to determine probation parole revocation supervision intensity or conditions of release through opaque risk scoring|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2831

```
TEC-JUD-062|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not assign supervision risk scores based on proxies for race class disability geography or protected activity|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2832

```
TEC-JUD-063|TEC|JUD|Probation and parole decisions must be based on individualized human review and may not be automated through behavioral prediction systems|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2833

```
TEC-JUD-064|TEC|JUD|AI may be used to identify patterns of bias inconsistency or unlawful disparity in probation and parole systems under transparent oversight|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2834

5. Fines, Fees, and Collections
```

### Excerpt 2835

```
TEC-JUD-065|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to escalate fines fees collections or penalties based on predictive payment scoring or behavioral profiling|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2836

```
TEC-JUD-066|TEC|JUD|Courts and governments may not use AI to pressure payment compliance through opaque threat scoring or automated penalty escalation|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2837

```
TEC-JUD-067|TEC|JUD|AI systems may not be used to convert administrative debt or missed payments into harsher legal consequences without direct human review and due process|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2838

```
TEC-JUD-068|TEC|JUD|AI may be used to identify unlawful disparities or predatory fine-and-fee patterns for corrective review under public oversight|MISSING

This is less dramatic, but very important. Search, classification, and docket systems can create invisible bias.

### Excerpt 2841

```
TEC-JUD-073|TEC|JUD|AI systems used to classify search summarize or prioritize court records must be auditable and may not distort case visibility or public access|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2842

```
TEC-JUD-074|TEC|JUD|Court administrative AI systems may not create unequal access to filing scheduling or record retrieval based on hidden prioritization logic|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2843

```
TEC-JUD-075|TEC|JUD|Public court records systems using AI must preserve accuracy transparency and the ability to correct errors promptly|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 2844

```
TEC-JUD-076|TEC|JUD|Courts must maintain human override and review mechanisms for all AI-assisted administrative systems affecting litigants or public access|MISSING

### Excerpt 3020

```
7. Immediate recommendations
```

### Excerpt 3021

```
These systems often function as delayed incarceration traps. They need to be treated as liberty-restricting systems, not light-touch administrative afterthoughts.
```

### Excerpt 3022

```
JUS-SUP-001|JUS|SUP|Probation and parole systems must be designed to support reintegration and public safety rather than act as pipelines back into incarceration|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3023

```
JUS-SUP-002|JUS|SUP|Conditions of probation and parole must be narrowly tailored proportionate and limited to what is necessary for safety or lawful supervision|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3024

```
JUS-SUP-003|JUS|SUP|Technical violations that do not involve new serious criminal conduct should not by default trigger incarceration|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3025

```
JUS-SUP-004|JUS|SUP|Revocation of probation or parole must require meaningful due process individualized review and proportionality|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3026

```
JUS-SUP-005|JUS|SUP|Probation and parole systems must not impose impossible financial travel employment or reporting conditions that function as traps for failure|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3027

```
JUS-SUP-006|JUS|SUP|Supervision terms should be periodically reviewed and shortened where continued supervision is not justified by clear evidence|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3028

```
2. Fines, fees, and monetary punishment
```

### Excerpt 3029

```
These systems frequently criminalize poverty and create coercive revenue extraction.
```

### Excerpt 3030

```
JUS-FFF-001|JUS|FFF|Justice systems must not use fines fees and monetary penalties in ways that criminalize poverty or create coercive revenue extraction|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3031

```
JUS-FFF-002|JUS|FFF|Ability to pay must be assessed before imposing or enforcing fines fees restitution schedules or other monetary penalties|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3032

```
JUS-FFF-003|JUS|FFF|People may not be incarcerated detained or otherwise deprived of liberty solely because of inability to pay fines or fees|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3033

```
JUS-FFF-004|JUS|FFF|Late fees interest penalties and compounding charges in justice-related debts must be strictly limited or prohibited|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3034

```
JUS-FFF-005|JUS|FFF|Courts and agencies must provide accessible alternatives including waiver reduction community-based alternatives or income-based payment structures|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3035

```
JUS-FFF-006|JUS|FFF|Justice institutions may not depend on fines fees or civil forfeiture as core operating revenue sources|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3036

```
3. Expungement, sealing, and record clearing
```

### Excerpt 3037

```
JUS-REC-001|JUS|REC|People should have broad access to sealing expungement and record-clearing processes so past contact with the justice system does not create lifelong exclusion|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3038

```
JUS-REC-002|JUS|REC|Expungement and sealing should be automatic in many categories rather than dependent on expensive complex petition processes|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3039

```
JUS-REC-003|JUS|REC|Juvenile records should be sealed or expunged automatically in most cases absent extraordinary circumstances|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3040

```
JUS-REC-004|JUS|REC|Dismissed charges acquittals and invalidated convictions should be removed or sealed promptly and without procedural barriers|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3041

```
JUS-REC-005|JUS|REC|Background-check systems and public records systems must honor sealing and expungement and may not recreate protected records through aggregation or inference|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3042

```
JUS-REC-006|JUS|REC|People must have accessible processes to correct errors in criminal-history records background reports and public databases|MISSING
```

```

```

### Excerpt 3045

```
JUS-RST-002|JUS|RST|Diversion programs should be expanded to reduce unnecessary criminalization especially for low-level offenses youth and nonviolent conduct|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3046

```

### Excerpt 3047

```

### Excerpt 3048

### Excerpt 3049

5. Collateral consequences and reintegration
```

### Excerpt 3050

```
JUS-PRP-001|JUS|PRP|Civil forfeiture should be banned or strictly limited and may not be used to deprive people of property without strong due process protections|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3051

```
JUS-PRP-002|JUS|PRP|Property deprivation connected to justice systems must require clear evidentiary standards timely review and meaningful opportunity to contest|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3052

```
JUS-PRP-003|JUS|PRP|Justice agencies may not rely on forfeiture or seized assets as operating revenue or direct financial incentive|MISSING

### Excerpt 3053

7. Process fairness after conviction
```

### Excerpt 3054

```
JUS-REV-005|JUS|REV|Post-conviction review should not be restricted by artificial deadlines where credible evidence of innocence legal error or scientific unreliability emerges later|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3055

```
JUS-REV-006|JUS|REV|People seeking post-conviction relief must have meaningful access to counsel records forensic review and procedural guidance|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3056

```
JUS-REV-007|JUS|REV|Justice systems should create presumptive review pathways when systemic misconduct or discredited methods affect classes of convictions|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3057

```
Why these additions matter
```

### Excerpt 3058

```
These rules fill a major structural weakness that exists in a lot of justice reform efforts: they focus not just on arrest and sentencing, but on the entire life-cycle of justice-system harm, including:
```

### Excerpt 3059

```
what happens after conviction
```

### Excerpt 3060

```
what poverty does inside legal systems
```

### Excerpt 3061

```
how long records keep hurting people
```

### Excerpt 3062

```
how supervision becomes a hidden punishment system
```

### Excerpt 3063

```
how alternatives to incarceration should actually work
```

```
Phase 2

### Excerpt 3068

misconduct records and evidence disclosure

These two rules together create a stronger system, but they imply:

```

```

without creating shortcuts around defendants’ rights or evidentiary fairness
```

### Excerpt 3078

```
JUS-VIC-001|JUS|VIC|Victims of crime must have access to protection information support services and meaningful participation without undermining due process or fair trial rights|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3079

```
JUS-VIC-002|JUS|VIC|Victim-support systems should include trauma-informed services healthcare counseling housing support and legal navigation where appropriate|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3080

```
JUS-VIC-003|JUS|VIC|Victims’ rights frameworks may not be used to erode evidentiary standards confrontation rights or the presumption of innocence|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3081

```
JUS-VIC-004|JUS|VIC|Victim participation must be structured to support justice and healing without converting prosecutors or courts into instruments of retribution alone|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3082

```
JUS-VIC-005|JUS|VIC|Access to victim-support services must be equitable and not depend on wealth geography race citizenship or media attention|MISSING

### Excerpt 3083

```
2. Witness protections and anti-intimidation
```

### Excerpt 3084

```
This is a major structural accountability gap in many systems.
```

### Excerpt 3085

```
JUS-POL-013|JUS|POL|Law enforcement misconduct records relevant to credibility honesty bias excessive force or constitutional violations must be preserved disclosed and made available where legally relevant|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3086

```
JUS-POL-014|JUS|POL|Prosecutors must disclose credibility-related misconduct records of law enforcement witnesses and may not suppress Brady or Giglio material|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3087

```
JUS-POL-015|JUS|POL|Police officers with substantiated records of dishonesty evidence tampering bias or repeated rights violations must face enforceable consequences including limits on testimony and decertification where appropriate|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3088

```
JUS-POL-016|JUS|POL|Law enforcement agencies may not use internal secrecy confidentiality agreements or record-destruction practices to conceal misconduct relevant to justice proceedings|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3089

```
JUS-POL-017|JUS|POL|Independent systems should track officer misconduct patterns decertification and credibility issues across jurisdictions to prevent silent rehiring and forum shopping|MISSING

### Excerpt 3090

4. Interpreter access and language justice
```

### Excerpt 3091

```
JUS-LNG-001|JUS|LNG|People involved in justice proceedings must have meaningful access to qualified interpretation and translation services at every critical stage|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3092

```
JUS-LNG-002|JUS|LNG|Interpreter and translation access must be provided without cost barriers to defendants witnesses victims and other necessary participants|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3093

```
JUS-LNG-003|JUS|LNG|Language access failures may not be treated as harmless error where they impair understanding participation defense or rights assertion|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3094

```
JUS-LNG-004|JUS|LNG|Critical justice proceedings may not rely solely on automated translation systems where accuracy or rights could be materially affected|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3095

```
JUS-LNG-005|JUS|LNG|Justice institutions must proactively identify language-access needs and may not place the burden entirely on affected individuals to navigate the system alone|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3096

```
5. Evidence disclosure and process fairness expansion
```

### Excerpt 3097

```
JUS-CRT-001|JUS|CRT|Justice systems must be designed for intelligibility and access so people can understand proceedings obligations rights and deadlines without needing insider knowledge|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3098

```
JUS-CRT-002|JUS|CRT|Courts must provide accessible notice scheduling and participation systems that do not punish people for disability poverty language barriers caregiving or lack of transportation|MISSING
```

### Excerpt 3099

```

### Excerpt 3722

```
🧠 Core principle
```

### Excerpt 3723

```
TAX-COR-004 | Corporate tax systems must reflect real economic activity and may not be reduced through artificial accounting, legal structuring, or financial engineering.
```

### Excerpt 3724

```
🚫 Loophole elimination (core rules)
```

### Excerpt 3725

```
TAX-LOP-001 | Tax provisions that primarily enable avoidance, deferral, or artificial reduction of tax liability without corresponding public benefit must be eliminated.
```

### Excerpt 3726

```
TAX-LOP-002 | Corporate tax law must be regularly reviewed to identify and remove emergent loopholes, avoidance schemes, and unintended gaps.
```

### Excerpt 3727

```
🧾 Deductions and write-offs
```

### Excerpt 3728

```
Limit abusive deductions
```

### Excerpt 3729

```
TAX-DED-001 | Deductions must be directly tied to legitimate business activity and may not be used to artificially reduce taxable income through unrelated or inflated expenses.
```

### Excerpt 3730

```
TAX-DED-002 | Excessive executive compensation, stock-based compensation, and similar mechanisms may be limited in deductibility where they function as tax avoidance tools.
```

### Excerpt 3731

```
TAX-DED-003 | Interest deductions may be limited where used to artificially shift profits or create internal debt structures for tax avoidance.
```

### Excerpt 3732

```
Anti-financial engineering
```

### Excerpt 3733

```
TAX-DED-004 | Internal financial arrangements, including intercompany loans, royalties, and licensing structures, may not be used to shift profits or erode the tax base.
```

### Excerpt 3734

```
TAX-DED-005 | Transactions lacking economic substance beyond tax reduction must be disallowed.
```

### Excerpt 3735

```
🌍 Profit shifting & base erosion (tightening your earlier rules)
```

### Excerpt 3736

```
TAX-BEP-001 | Corporate income must be taxed based on real economic activity, including sales, workforce, and physical presence, rather than legal entity location alone.
```

### Excerpt 3737

```
TAX-BEP-002 | Transfer pricing must reflect real market value and may not be manipulated to shift profits across jurisdictions.
```

### Excerpt 3738

```
TAX-BEP-003 | Artificial profit allocation to low-tax jurisdictions without corresponding real activity is prohibited.
```

### Excerpt 3739

```
📊 Minimum effective taxation
```

### Excerpt 3740

```
TAX-COR-005 | Corporations must pay a minimum effective tax rate based on real economic activity regardless of deductions, credits, or structural arrangements.
```

### Excerpt 3741

```
TAX-COR-006 | Effective tax rate calculations must account for global income and may not be reduced through jurisdictional fragmentation.
```

### Excerpt 3742

```
💸 Stock buybacks & extraction
```

### Excerpt 3743

```
TAX-EXT-001 | Stock buybacks and similar financial extraction mechanisms may be subject to taxation or limitation where they do not contribute to productive investment.
```

### Excerpt 3744

```
TAX-EXT-002 | Tax policy may not incentivize financial engineering, short-term shareholder extraction, or non-productive capital allocation.
```

### Excerpt 3745

```
🧠 Corporate behavior alignment
```

### Excerpt 3746

```
TAX-INC-004 | Corporate tax structures should reward long-term investment, worker compensation, domestic reinvestment, and public-benefit outcomes.
```

### Excerpt 3747

```
TAX-INC-005 | Corporate tax incentives may not reward offshoring, wage suppression, or environmental harm.
```

### Excerpt 3748

```
🔍 Transparency & reporting
```

### Excerpt 3749

```
TAX-TRN-004 | Large corporations must publicly report revenue, profit, tax paid, workforce, and activity by jurisdiction.
```

### Excerpt 3750

```
TAX-TRN-005 | Corporate structures, subsidiaries, and ownership chains must be fully disclosed for tax and regulatory purposes.
```

### Excerpt 3771

```
Tax — Personal Taxes, Payroll, and Simplification
```

### Excerpt 3772

```
Core simplification principle
```

### Excerpt 3773

```
TAX-SMP-004 | Tax systems for ordinary individuals must be simple, low-burden, understandable, and designed so compliance does not require professional assistance in routine cases.
```

### Excerpt 3774

```
TAX-SMP-008 | Individuals with straightforward income, withholding, and deduction profiles should not be required to navigate complex filing systems or purchase commercial tax-preparation products.
```

### Excerpt 3775

```
TAX-SMP-009 | The government must provide free public filing systems that are easy to use, transparent, and available to all taxpayers.
```

### Excerpt 3776

```
TAX-SMP-010 | Public tax-filing systems must be designed to minimize error, maximize transparency, and clearly show how tax obligations are calculated.
```

### Excerpt 3777

```
TAX-PER-001 | Personal income tax systems must be progressive overall and structured so those with greater ability to pay contribute more.
```

### Excerpt 3778

```
TAX-PER-003 | Personal tax systems must protect low-income households from tax burdens that undermine basic living standards.
```

### Excerpt 3779

```
TAX-PAY-001 | Payroll and labor-related tax systems must be structured so they do not disproportionately burden workers relative to owners of concentrated wealth and passive capital.
```

### Excerpt 3780

```
TAX-PAY-002 | Payroll tax systems should be reviewed and rebalanced where necessary to reduce undue burden on wage earners and smaller employers.
```

### Excerpt 3781

```
TAX-PAY-003 | Funding for social insurance systems must remain stable and durable while ensuring the contribution structure is fair across labor and capital income.
```

### Excerpt 3782

```
TAX-PER-005 | Common deductions and credits with broad public purpose should be incorporated into simple filing systems automatically wherever feasible.
```

### Excerpt 3783

```
This is where the system gets sharper.
```

### Excerpt 3784

```
TAX-WTH-008 | Tax systems must apply heightened reporting, audit, and anti-avoidance scrutiny to high-net-worth individuals and complex personal wealth structures.
```

### Excerpt 3785

```
TAX-SMP-013 | Public tax systems must be accessible to people with disabilities, limited English proficiency, low digital literacy, and limited financial sophistication.
```

### Excerpt 3786

```
TAX-SMP-014 | Tax notices, filings, and payment systems must be written in clear language and supported by accessible public assistance.
```

### Excerpt 3787

```
TAX-ADM-001 | Tax administration systems should use automation, data matching, and modern technology to simplify filing for ordinary taxpayers and improve enforcement against complex evasion.
```

### Excerpt 3788

```
TAX-ADM-002 | Automated tax-administration systems must be transparent, reviewable, and subject to human appeal where they materially affect taxpayer obligations or enforcement outcomes.
```

### Excerpt 3789

```
TAX-DMJ-003 | The tax system must be visibly fair in practice so that ordinary taxpayers are not asked to comply with burdens from which wealthy actors routinely escape.
```

### Excerpt 3790

```
TAX-DMJ-004 | Simplicity, fairness, and enforceability are co-equal design goals of the tax system and may not be traded away in favor of complexity that benefits powerful interests.
```

### Excerpt 3791

```
normal people hate taxes because filing is miserable
```

### Excerpt 3792

```
wealthy people game the system through complexity
```

### Excerpt 3793

```
enforcement gets politically delegitimized
```

### Excerpt 3794

```
`ADM-SYS-001 | Administrative agencies are legitimate constitutional instruments of democratic governance and must exist to implement, enforce, and adapt public-interest law in areas requiring expertise, continuity, and active oversight.`
```

### Excerpt 3795

```
`ADM-SYS-002 | Agencies must be strong enough to regulate complex modern systems effectively, but constrained by transparency, accountability, review, and anti-capture protections.`
```

### Excerpt 3796

```
`ADM-SYS-003 | Agency design must prevent both political sabotage and unaccountable bureaucratic abuse.`
```

### Excerpt 3797

```
`ADM-FND-001 | Agencies charged with protecting rights, public safety, markets, health, labor, environment, or democratic systems must receive stable and adequate funding sufficient to carry out their lawful mission.`
```

### Excerpt 3798

```
`ADM-ENF-003 | Repeated or systemic violations must trigger escalated review and stronger remedies rather than routine settlement or symbolic penalties alone.`
```

### Excerpt 3799

```
`ADM-ENF-004 | Enforcement systems may not rely solely on fines where ongoing behavior requires operational, structural, or leadership remedies.`
```

### Excerpt 3800

```
`ADM-ADJ-001 | Agency adjudication systems must provide clear notice, records access, explanation of decisions, and meaningful appeal rights.`
```

### Excerpt 3801

```
`ADM-OVR-003 | Findings of systemic agency failure, capture, abuse, or chronic non-enforcement must trigger mandatory corrective review.`
```

### Excerpt 3802

```
`TAX-IRS-002 | IRS modernization must prioritize easy filing for ordinary taxpayers, high-end enforcement, anti-fraud capacity, and modern data systems.`
```

### Excerpt 3803

```
## Filing and public systems
```

### Excerpt 3804

```
`TAX-IRS-003 | The IRS must provide free, direct, public tax filing systems for individuals and small businesses.`
```

### Excerpt 3805

```
`TAX-IRS-005 | Taxpayer-facing systems must be accessible, multilingual, disability-compatible, and usable without specialized tax software.`
```

### Excerpt 3806

```
`TAX-IRS-006 | IRS enforcement systems must prioritize sophisticated high-revenue avoidance and evasion rather than focusing primarily on low-income, low-complexity cases.`
```

### Excerpt 3807

```
`TAX-IRS-007 | IRS data systems should support detection of shell structures, beneficial ownership concealment, high-risk transfer patterns, and abusive complexity.`
```

### Excerpt 3808

```
`TAX-IRS-010 | Tax administration must visibly demonstrate that high-wealth and high-complexity cases are enforced seriously in order to preserve legitimacy of the tax system.`
```

### Excerpt 3809

```
`FIN-SYS-001 | Public finance must prioritize durable funding of essential public goods, long-term resilience, and reduction of systemic risk.`
```

### Excerpt 3810

```
`FIN-SYS-002 | Budget policy may not rely on chronic underinvestment in essential systems while preserving tax advantages or subsidies for concentrated private gain.`
```

### Excerpt 3811

```
`FIN-PRI-001 | Public budgets must prioritize healthcare, education, housing, labor enforcement, environmental protection, infrastructure, disability access, democratic systems, and public safety rooted in rights and accountability.`
```

### Excerpt 3812

```
`FIN-PRI-003 | Essential rights-protecting and public-interest systems may not be systematically weakened through austerity where doing so predictably creates larger long-term harm.`
```

### Excerpt 3824

```
TAX-ENV-001 | Environmental tax policy must internalize environmental harms that are currently externalized onto the public, future generations, and ecosystems.
```

### Excerpt 3825

```
TAX-ENV-002 | Environmental taxation must be structured to reduce pollution, waste, emissions, ecosystem damage, and resource depletion while supporting a just transition.
```

### Excerpt 3826

```
TAX-ENV-003 | Environmental taxes and fees may not be used as substitutes for direct regulation where direct regulation is necessary to prevent serious harm.
```

### Excerpt 3827

```
TAX-ENV-004 | Carbon-intensive activity may be subject to direct taxation or equivalent pricing mechanisms based on real, auditable emissions and lifecycle impact.
```

### Excerpt 3828

```
TAX-ENV-005 | Environmental tax systems must not rely on unverifiable offsets, accounting games, or market abstractions that allow polluters to avoid real reductions.
```

### Excerpt 3829

```
TAX-ENV-006 | Carbon pricing or equivalent environmental taxation must be tied to measurable emissions, sectoral risk, and enforceable reduction obligations.
```

### Excerpt 3830

```
TAX-ENV-007 | Environmental tax systems must distinguish between productive necessity and avoidable pollution while preserving a clear path toward decarbonization.
```

### Excerpt 3831

```
TAX-ENV-008 | Water extraction and intensive water use may be subject to taxation, fees, or escalating charges where use patterns threaten sustainability, public access, or ecosystem health.
```

### Excerpt 3832

```
TAX-ENV-009 | Water-related taxation and pricing must account for local scarcity, watershed stress, aquifer depletion, and downstream ecological impact.
```

### Excerpt 3833

```
TAX-ENV-010 | Large-scale industrial water use may be subject to enhanced reporting, monitoring, and environmental contribution requirements.
```

### Excerpt 3834

```
TAX-ENV-011 | Water taxation and fee systems must not undermine basic household access to safe water or create hardship for essential personal use.
```

### Excerpt 3835

```
TAX-ENV-012 | Polluting activities and persistent pollutants may be subject to taxes, fees, or strict liability charges proportionate to the harm they create.
```

### Excerpt 3836

```
TAX-ENV-013 | Tax and fee systems may be used to discourage plastics, toxic byproducts, non-recyclable packaging, persistent waste, and environmentally destructive production methods.
```

### Excerpt 3837

```
TAX-ENV-014 | Environmental taxes must account for lifecycle harm, including extraction, production, use, disposal, and leakage into ecosystems.
```

### Excerpt 3838

```
TAX-ENV-015 | Environmental tax policy should incentivize durable products, repairability, recyclability, material recovery, and reduced waste generation.
```

### Excerpt 3839

```
TAX-ENV-016 | Tax advantages may be used to support repair, remanufacturing, refurbishment, reuse systems, and circular-economy infrastructure.
```

### Excerpt 3840

```
TAX-ENV-017 | Tax policy may disfavor disposable, non-repairable, short-lifecycle, or environmentally persistent products where viable alternatives exist.
```

### Excerpt 3841

```
TAX-ENV-018 | Environmental tax systems must include strong anti-evasion rules so firms may not avoid liability through offshoring, shell structures, accounting manipulation, or supply-chain opacity.
```

### Excerpt 3842

```
TAX-ENV-019 | Corporations may not reduce environmental tax obligations through paper relocation or outsourcing of pollution without corresponding real reductions in harm.
```

### Excerpt 3843

```
TAX-ENV-020 | Imports and cross-border supply chains may be subject to border-adjustment or equivalent mechanisms where necessary to prevent pollution offshoring and regulatory arbitrage.
```

### Excerpt 3844

```
TAX-ENV-021 | Revenue from environmental taxation should support environmental cleanup, climate resilience, public health protection, ecosystem restoration, and just-transition programs.
```

### Excerpt 3845

```
TAX-ENV-022 | Environmental tax revenue should also support household cost relief, public transportation, energy transition, and adaptation for communities most affected by environmental harm.
```

### Excerpt 3846

```
TAX-ENV-023 | Environmental tax systems must be designed so that working households and low-income communities are not unfairly burdened relative to major industrial polluters and concentrated private gain.
```

### Excerpt 3847

```
TAX-ENV-024 | Environmental tax systems must be transparent, publicly understandable, and based on standardized reporting, measurable criteria, and auditable data.
```

### Excerpt 3848

```
TAX-ENV-025 | Entities subject to environmental taxes or fees must file standardized environmental tax disclosures using defined forms, metrics, and reporting periods.
```

### Excerpt 3849

```
TAX-ENV-026 | Environmental tax enforcement must coordinate with environmental regulators, public auditors, and enforcement agencies to detect fraud, underreporting, and collusion.
```

### Excerpt 3850

```
TAX-ENV-027 | Fraud, concealment, collusion, or knowing misreporting in environmental tax filings or environmental impact disclosures must trigger significant civil and criminal penalties.
```

### Excerpt 3851

```
TAX-ENV-028 | Auditors, executives, officers, and responsible individuals may be held civilly and criminally liable for fraudulent environmental tax reporting, concealment, or conspiracy.
```

### Excerpt 3852

```
TAX-ENV-029 | Repeated or systemic environmental tax violations must trigger escalating penalties, corrective orders, audit expansion, and structural remedies where necessary.
```
